_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers)_

### REQ: Fujitsu COBOL print preview

- **From:** "R.P." <palsg@hotmail.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-05T15:59:34
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<90j3f7$5tt$1@venus.telepac.pt>`

```
    I need an ActiveX to use with Fujitsu COBOL 5.0 that can preview and
print documents.
    I 'm using Fujitsu PowerForm, but it can only print documents.
thanks
```

#### ↳ Re: Fujitsu COBOL print preview

- **From:** "Jerry P" <jerryp@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-09T17:53:34+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ykuY5.5367$U4.551865@newsread1.prod.itd.earthlink.net>`
- **References:** `<90j3f7$5tt$1@venus.telepac.pt>`

```
I think the PRINT CONTROL can preview documents, but it's pretty
limited. PowerForm, too, has its limitations, one of which is print
preview (another is lack of TAB control, another is lack of selecting
a printer, another is, oh well...).

Frankly, I despair of you ever finding what you want. It would seem
that an ActiveX print preview control would have had to be the one
that created the printout being previewed. That is, I can't imagine
how an ActiveX component could interact with PowerForm (PF is
(probably) not OLE compliant).

If you find one, let me know.


"R.P." <palsg@hotmail.com> wrote in message
news:90j3f7$5tt$1@venus.telepac.pt...

and
> print documents.
>     I 'm using Fujitsu PowerForm, but it can only print documents.
> thanks
>
>
```

## Thread: Need articles and books on coding standards — 7 msgs

_Topics: [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards) · [`Tutorials, books, learning`](topics.md#learning)_

### Need articles and books on coding standards

- **From:** luke <luke_bryan@my-deja.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-07T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<90p4bi$855$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`

```
I've been assigned to a COBOL coding standards committee.
I know that many coding standards projects have failed for one reason
or another.

Please email me and let me know where I can find books and articles
about COBOL coding standards.  I'm most interested in real life
examples of what worked and what didn't.

luke_bryan@my-deja.com


Sent via Deja.com http://www.deja.com/
Before you buy.
```

#### ↳ Re: Need articles and books on coding standards

- **From:** rjones1005@aol.com (RJones1005)
- **Date:** 2000-12-08T01:06:19+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<20001207200619.14301.00000069@ng-fw1.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<90p4bi$855$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`

```
I recently purchased "Code Complete".  Even though It does not talk about COBOL
per se  ( it mentions FORTRAN, C, C++, Pascal.. but not COBOL) I wish I had
read it years ago (came out in 1995 I think).  I think it's worth your
attention.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Need articles and books on coding standards

- **From:** rjones1005@aol.com (RJones1005)
- **Date:** 2000-12-15T04:38:30+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<20001214233830.10582.00001463@ng-fn1.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<60XX5.4316$Sl.224178@iad-read.news.verio.net>`

```
><luke_bryan@my-deja.com> wrote:
>>I've been assigned to a COBOL coding standards committee.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>

A good place to start is with your own programmers.  I'm sure you will get some
nuggets from the group.  And perhaps most importantly, THEY are the ones who
have to live with them - best to get them involved, perhaps getting a small set
of "guidelines" they can live with to get used to the idea.

Many coding standards are common sense (but not necessarily in common use) and
one book you might read is "code complete" by Steve McConnell.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Need articles and books on coding standards

- **From:** floydj@netins.net (Floyd Johnson)
- **Date:** 2000-12-15T15:36:26+00:00

```
Another book that parallels CODE COMPLETE is "The Practice of
Programming" by Kernighan and Pike.  The examples are in C (Kernighan was
partialy responsible for the development of the C language), but the
principles are valid across multiple languages.
```

## Thread: Acucorp is proud to announce the release of extend 5.1 with ACUCOBOL-GT — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers)_

### Acucorp is proud to announce the release of extend 5.1 with ACUCOBOL-GT

- **From:** Jay Reilly <jreilly@acucorp.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-07T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A2FD84B.4BE75BE1@acucorp.com>`

```
Acucorp, Inc., an international provider of legacy extension solutions
for COBOL applications, is proud to announce the release of extend 5.1
with ACUCOBOL-GT. The extend 5.1 family offers the most comprehensive
set of technologies for transporting inherited applications into modern
systems. These solutions include Internet deployment, graphical COBOL
transitions, data access, distributed computing, and programmer
productivity. 

extend 5.1 with ACUCOBOL-GT offers some significant new features and
capabilities that help to make the development process easier and faster
for COBOL programmers. Some of the new features in 5.1 include: 
  


*	Character-to-GUI Wizard
*	Heightened plug-in security
*	Extended support for ActiveX and OLE Objects
*	Bundle configuration files with helper application library
*	Oracle 8i support
*	Redesigned AcuOBDC configuration interface
*	New multi-record mode in AcuServer
*	General Preprocessor Interface
*	Support for error and exit procedures
*	File I/O redirect facility
*	Numerous enhancements to AcuBench?, including;
*	Ability to Build or Rebuild just one project in the open
workspace
*	Screen Designer for graphical and character-based screen
creation
*	Simplified Version Control configuration interface
*	COBOL-intelligent source Code Generator


For more information on the extend 5.1, contact Acucorp at
1-800-262-6585 - Fax: 858-689-4550 - E-mail: info@acucorp.com -
Internet: http://www.acucorp.com <http://www.acucorp.com> .
```

<a id="t-3db09ac78d"></a>
## Thread: CD 1.10 inconsistency — 3 msgs

### CD 1.10 inconsistency

- **From:** Herwig Huener <Herwig.Huener!@!fujitsu-siemens.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-07T16:25:07+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<90oa91$1pp$1@news.mch.sbs.de>`

```
2000-12-07 16:25:36 MEZ

In CD 1.10 in section C.23 Example of
free form reference format you see that
inline comments are legal in either free
or fixed reference format. The same rule
follows from 6.1.2 or 6.2.6.2.

In 7.2.2 Syntax rules, bullet 3 and 4
clearly indicate that, for directives,
inline comments are allowed only in
free reference format.

It cannot be that directives have a
restriction which ordinary COBOL-lines
have not. However, we implemented all
that already.

:-(

I expect that the said restriction
for directives will be taken away in
the final standard, and that
inline-comments are allowed in either
reference formats, for directives as
well as ordinary program-text.

So I make inline-comments legal in
directives in either format right now.

Or is there good reason not to do so?

Herwig
```

#### ↳ Re: CD 1.10 inconsistency

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-07T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<90ontc$77k$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<90oa91$1pp$1@news.mch.sbs.de>`

```
I don't know why you post this type of question in this NG and do not send
them to J4 - but FYI,  there were specify "debates" on where in-line
comments would and would not be allowed and I *doubt* that the current rules
will be changed. (i.e. I do NOT expect them to be allowed in fixed form
compiler directing statements)

FYI,
  At the last meeting (and I don't know if this is or is not reflected in CD
1.10) there was a "new" restriction added that inline comments are NOT
allowed on a line with a continued alphanumeric literal, e.g the following
is non-conforming.

    05 abc Pic X(100) Value "abc"-  *> this isn't allowed
           "xyz".
```

```
I know most of you are professional programmers either in COBOL or another
language, but when you guys were first learning to program did u get
discouraged?  I am a CIS major Freshman, and right now i am so discourage i
feel like changing my major.  I work so hard trying to get everything right
then i go into class thinking ok i am prepared i studied and everything is
looking good, then i walk out with a 2/5 on the quiz.  I am starting to think
programming isnt for me, i love computers and the internet, but I aint the
smartest when it comes to math and stuff, but I work hard at everything I do, i
am going to end up with like a C in the class and its like agh, all that work

Derrick
```

#### ↳ Re: discouraged with programming

- **From:** "Pete McBreen, McBreen.Consulting" <mcbreenp@cadvision.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-08T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<srYX5.191050$76.3782533@news1.rdc1.ab.home.com>`
- **References:** `<20001207204635.14613.00000056@ng-fw1.aol.com>`

```
Deek40 wrote in message
<20001207204635.14613.00000056@ng-fw1.aol.com>...
>I know most of you are professional programmers either in COBOL or
another
>language, but when you guys were first learning to program did u get
>discouraged?

It's easy to get discouraged by quizzes, I think that's why instructors
like them so much:-)

Hopefully though your instructor is explaining the answers and _how it
is possible to get tricked into picking the wrong answer_

What you have to remember is that programming is not the same as
answering a short quiz. If you can write good code and can maintain and
enhance existing programs nobody is going to care what grade you got (as
long as you still manage to pass). After all, writing a 10,000 line
COBOL program is an open book problem where you are allowed to
collaborate with your colleagues, and you can keep on working until it
passes all of the acceptance tests.

To answer the other question, I found programming is hard until my mind
decided it could do it. Every time I learn a new programming language
(about 1 per year for the last 17 years) I go through a phase of utter
confusion where nothing seems to work right, then just before I decide
to give up everything clicks and the new language becomes really easy to
learn. I've now decided that this is the way my mind learns, it dives
deep into confusion until it manages to make the appropriate connections
between all of the new stuff, then it's easy

Pete
----
Pete McBreen, McBreen.Consulting , Cochrane, AB
email: petemcbreen@acm.org    http://www.mcbreen.ab.ca/
Software development is meant to be fun,
     if it isn't the process is wrong
```

```
Derrick writes ...

-stuff I annotated below. 

Derrick, programming is a job that requires attention to detail, almost a
fussiness, to get it right. I find your note to be careless in terms of
punctuation, spelling, and grammar. To master a language, whether it's COBOL or
English, you need to pay attention to details, and it doesn't matter if it's
"just a usenet posting", it represents your respect for the language and the
audience. Furthermore, if your writing is code, it won't work.

Being smart about math is not relevant. There are plenty of excellent
programmers who have no love of math. It's the logical / methodical approach
that makes good mathematicians and good programmers, but the subject matter is
typically different.


>I know most of you are professional programmers either in COBOL or another
>language, but when you guys were first learning to program did u get
>discouraged?  

"u"? This kind of usage makes me think maybe you would prefer C as a
programming language as opposed to, say, Assembler or COBOL.

>i think this might be a first).

- I think this might be a first).

Now I'm sure a lot of my colleagues on this list think I'm being too pedantic.
But in my experience, this is the way the programming world works. "Working
hard" means paying attention to details. It doesn't mean getting everything
right, especially the first time, but it means approaching all tasks with this
attitude and then going back and fixing the errors as they are discovered.

Best wishes in your career choices.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: discouraged with programming

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-08T10:11:19-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<90r12a$5nh$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<20001207204635.14613.00000056@ng-fw1.aol.com> <20001208103146.22551.00000247@ng-fh1.aol.com>`

```
One slight modification that I would add/make to what Steve says is that
MUCH of programming has to "getting it right in the end".  This means that
it is NOT critical that your first attempt be perfect, but that you need to
be able to "debug" your code when it isn't doing what it should.  (Similar
to using proofreading tools when sending out notes on usenet).

I know that you (Derrick) have asked several questions about code that you
have created and were having problems with.  My question to you is "Are you
getting a 'feeling' on how to find the solutions yourself?"

I have never "judged" a programmer on their ability to create 100% perfect
code on their first attempt - but I do look at how they try and solve the
problems that come up with their first (and subsequent) attempts.  To me,
this is the MOST important thing that you can be learning from your
programming classes.  I do NOT want you to think that you shouldn't keep
asking us for help - most of your questions have been reasonable and you
have given us all the information that we needed to help you.  HOWEVER, I
would ask you to judge yourself on whether you are understanding your
problems better - and figuring out "techniques" for fixing your problems
yourself.  If you miss questions on a quiz/test - I don't consider that
crucial.  On the other hand, if you are getting frustrated with not being
able to "solve" your own programming problems time after time, that may
indicate more of a problem.  On the third <G> hand, I am certain that EVERY
programmer in this NG can tell you bugs that they have spent "weeks" on that
(once they found them) seemed "obvious."  So that is part of the profession
as well.
```

- **References:** `<90r12a$5nh$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20001208134645.05994.00003772@ng-ck1.aol.com>`

```
Deek40 wrote:
> 
> I am the type of person if you show me how to do something once I can do it,
> but my teacher shows us after he grades us on the stuff.  I can't teach myself
> programming then have the test, then have the teacher teach it.  If it was
> Histort or Economics I could learn that way, but not COBOL.

I am also a Quick Learner, in fact I majored in Business while in
College and became a systems analyst by persistently asking the *right*
questions about my computer generated reports and applications.

Your teacher is trying to get you to think about the problem before he
(or she) presents the solution.  If you have the answer, you have not
thrashed around first and solved it for yourself.

It also helps to be very, very picky about details.  Some of the most
heated arguments on this NewsGroup are about Style.  In the end your
coding style does not matter that much if you are consistent.  All the
really excellent programmers I know are nearly boring in their
application of consistent coding style.  This predictability makes
debugging their code a pleasure for the analyst or for themselves should
changes be required, or for the follow in programmer who has learned
this programmer always names his paragraphs this way, always uses his
(or her) variables that way, always comments in the paragraphs (or at
the top of the program) and so forth.

As with any new skill, you feel lost a great deal in the beginning, but
it does become more comfortable over time.

For example (if you ski or snow board), your first experience was no
doubt a big failure and even if you are Olympic Class now, you had a lot
of bumps and falls in the bunny slope in the beginning as you learned
how to do it properly.

	Susan
```

```


Deek40 wrote:

> That was a very interesting note to read thanks for the reply.  I am 100% sure
> now i want to continue with programming, but I am not sure if I want to program
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Deek

The trouble is, programming has entered into lots of job descriptions.  When I
started working middle managers used dictaphones, upper managers used secretaries.
Now they need to be able to type.  Middle managers need to be able to write spread
sheets, and often write macros.  Accountants write queries and report programs.
Even musicians write programs.

Becoming a better programmer than your peers in non-programming fields can only
help you.

But decide what it is you like about computers.  Maybe you should get a part time
job at a computer store while continuing your education.   Maybe you would enjoy
working with LANs, TCP/IP, and communication.  You won't know until you actually
play with these choices.

Maybe you only think you want to work with computers.  I KNEW I wanted to be a
pilot.  Unfortunately, I didn't have the right stuff - I never stopped getting sick
in the Air Force.  I didn't have a back-up plan, but fortunately had the G.I. Bill
to give me time.   A computer education can get you into a business field, as they
all use computers.  So work on business skills, people skills, etc.  Maybe learn
the software which will get you a job in a company you like.
```

```
Deek it depends on what kind/type of computers you want to work with.
If you're interested in mainframes, you could always go into
operations.  You don't need to know programming, but having had the
classes would help you to put JCL (Job Control Language) together
should you be required.

If you're more interested in PC's then maybe you want to look at being
a network administrator.  You don't need to be a programmer to do that
either.  You need to understand LAN's, WAN's and the like and you can
become Novell certified and be employable.  I understand that some
universities are offering classes that lead to a Cisco certification
as well.  

Best thing to do is talk to your student counselor.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: discouraged with programming

- **From:** Steve Thompson <sthompson2_@neo.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-08T16:52:52+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A3111BA.F439AB0A@neo.rr.com>`
- **References:** `<20001207204635.14613.00000056@ng-fw1.aol.com> <20001208103146.22551.00000247@ng-fh1.aol.com>`

```
S Comstock wrote:
<snip>
> Now I'm sure a lot of my colleagues on this list think I'm being too pedantic.
> But in my experience, this is the way the programming world works. "Working
> hard" means paying attention to details. It doesn't mean getting everything
> right, especially the first time, but it means approaching all tasks with this
> attitude and then going back and fixing the errors as they are discovered.
<snip>

I for one do not think you are being "too pedantic."  While this
is an n/g for COBOL, the problem is, programming computers
requires that you think like a computer.  The syntax of a
language is not the same as programming, but is related.

What the original poster is doing today could be related to
chess.  They are learning how each piece moves, but, they have
not yet learned strategy.  Or, it could be related to flying. 
They are learning to land the plane, but, their ultimate goal is
to fly in the clouds by instruments -- meanwhile, the ground
school is pretty rigorous in discussing all the things you have
to know to just get to get your private license.

Now for my own adventures with computing.  I did not score very
well in the first semester of a two semester course on S/360
Assembly language.  However, I persisted and did quite well the
second semester.  And with more persistence, I became a systems
programmer, and then went on to become a software developer.

It took two things for me to get here.  (1) Persistence and (2)
thinking literally.

#2 drove those non-programmers, that I was around, nuts
(including my wife).  I forced myself to stop accepting idioms
and look at phrases for what the actually meant.  Example:  Hit
the lights.  The intent is, if they are off, turn them on, and if
they are on, turn them off.  By doing this, I learned more and
more about logic.  After a while, this came naturally and then I
went back and learned to speak to normal people (which pleased my
wife no end).

Programming is related to inventing:  1% inspiration and 99%
perspiration.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: discouraged with programming

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 2000-12-14T08:57:23-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<jd5_5.2729$QY4.90495@news2.atl>`
- **References:** `<20001207204635.14613.00000056@ng-fw1.aol.com> <20001208103146.22551.00000247@ng-fh1.aol.com> <3A3111BA.F439AB0A@neo.rr.com>`

```
I agree with Steve's comments.

In my first programming class, I was the last person, out of the 20
or so students, to 'get it'.  My problem was that my mental image of
what the computer was doing was confused by how movies and TV had
portrayed them.  I was going through the motions, but I didn't really
understand what and why.  When I finally figured out that computers
are really, really stupid, and that they simply did one simple
instruction after another, it was like a great revelation, and it
became much simpler for me.  Soon, programming became as easy for me
as breathing, and lots of fun.

Not everyone has the aptitude for programming.  But human nature in
general is such that if you enjoy doing something, it is a pretty
good indication that you have an aptitude for it.  It turned out for
me that I was a born programmer; I cannot imagine any field that
would suit me or my particular abilities as well as programming.
But learning programming was not natural at first, and it still took
a lot of work, though it was all fun after 'the light came on'.

Like Steve, after programming for a while, I began to notice that I
was developing a part of my brain that even college studies such as
calculus, geometry and logical proofs had not fully developed.  The
entire process of information assimilation and analysis for me was
becoming far more logical and precise.  My decision making abilities
were greatly enhanced and far more logical, all across the board.

Derrick, only you can decide whether or not programming is right for
you.  But just remember that, even if you do have the talent, it might
not be especially easy for you at first.  Learning to program well
requires a significant shift in some of your mental processes, if you
are like most people.  Programming is an extremely exacting and
severely logical process, and not everybody likes it or is well suited
to it.  My wife, for example, is a quite intelligent person, but she
would go bananas if forced to learn programming. :-)  She is a fine
artist, but the exacting logical detail required for programming,
which is fun for me, would be an anathema to her.  On the other hand,
not only can't I draw a straight line without a ruler, she trounces
me at Trivial Pursuit, and her portraits look almost photographic. :-)
Our thought process are so different that sometimes in the middle of a
heated discussion I ask myself "Are we having the same conversation?"
```

```
Steve, 
I really like your answer.  And I agree. Programming requires a diciplined
thought process and attention to detail. These requirements to successful
program development are often daunting to the novice. 

Derrick, do read each program assignment and immediately attempt to solve it
with COBOL.  Think about the steps of the program in English; and only after
you have completely stated the steps of the problem in sufficient detail, do
you begin translating it into COBOL.  In other words, you don't build a new car
by starting pushing sheetmetal through the assembly line - the auto is designed
first, specified how it will look, what it will do and how it will do it.  Then
they start building it.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: discouraged with programming

- **From:** "Warren Simmons" <warren.simmons@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2000-12-13T05:36:29+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<xVDZ5.6060$d62.443313@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<20001208103146.22551.00000247@ng-fh1.aol.com> <20001210140502.21282.00004256@ng-fd1.aol.com>`

```
On box.

The programming learning list is interesting because:

Not has changed in 40 plus years.

People learned how to time a car, wire a plug board, and several other
things over time at different rates.

My personal experience with learning to program included six weeks of eight
hour days of instructions on the 64 instructions of the computer with some
simple examples.

This was followed by six more weeks of detail logic flow  training on how
the Univac I handled these 64 instructions and other operating things one
had to learn.

This was followed by assignments where we were given the c-10 code for our
I/O, and restart procedures.

Following that someone who knew the applications would sketch out a chain
of runs for this tape system, and give us parts to code. No computer was
available.

At some point we were taken to the Pep
Boys Building in Philadelphia to use Univac I's on the production line to
learn to debug with a very knowledgeable senior from Univac.  He was so
qualified he had an engineering degree, and a math degree.  If our hardware
failed (and it often did) he would find parts on the other machines so we
continue.

He operated for us, and only because he showed us how we learned to set
break points, and ask the operator to do other things like single step the
program so we could follow the execution on our flow charts. Resources were
such that we had lots of time for all the preparation.

From those experiences we graduated to feeling our way around our own
machine that had been installed.  Our operator (one of the class) had gone
through many weeks of training at the factory but still was like a child in
a play pen relying upon (in the final analysis) the help of this same guy
who
helped us in Philadelphia.  He coded examples for us. We traded code that
worked.

From this group of twelve, several different ranges of ability developed.
Some who knew the applications and the unit record systems did not end up
coding at all.  They became project leaders.  Several teams were created,
and we began in earnest.  At the end of the first YEAR we had done some
simple things, and began to form the opinion that anyone being trained
would need about a YEAR  of experience in class, and in other forms to
become productive.  When asked to train a second class, we settled on a six
week training period, and seasoning.

With No standards, and differing methods our work was a can of worms, but
began to hang together.  Some did not Make Programmer.  It was not expected
that some of them would, but they needed to know the methods so they could
manage the resources for us.

So out of a dozen, we had about six programmers leading to subsequent
classes where in each case the training was "short sheeted" based upon the
value judgements of the interviews and those doing the interviewing.

The most sought after ability was the knowledge of how business systems
worked.  The next on those with some exposure to methods in other
environments who could be trained faster.

Since I lived through most of the above and some other training tasks, I
feel qualified to say that IMHO, no course given in today's higher level of
education   can begin to prepare most people beyond what might be called
cannon fodder.  Some of the worst were those trained using fixed decks of
cards, and math oriented problems with little access to the computer or
help from the instructor.

Because of self teachers I've seen, and the fact it's 40+ years later, some
better methods are used.  Where a students work is on business applications
for the school or school sponsors, the results are better.

To me, learning to program is a lot more than learning a language and
procedures for using some computer.  And as in any endeavor some of those
who train for this kind of career find that they feel better doing
something not directly associated with programming or computers.  It's like
some who go to med. school end up selling doctors on the latest drug a
company has.  And that's good.  A computer salesman who knows the
conditions of programming is much better prepared to sell his
computer/software, etc.

Off box.

Warren Simmons
```

- **From:** hankelie@my-deja.com

```
In article <20001207204635.14613.00000056@ng-fw1.aol.com>,
  deek40@aol.com (Deek40) wrote:
> I know most of you are professional programmers either in COBOL or
> another language, but when you guys were first learning to program
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> be a first).

Unhappy?  Depressed?  Then visit comp.lang.cobol... :-)

Seriously though, Derrick, it sounds like you're at the stage where
things haven't quite clicked.  It happened to most of us, I suspect,
where you learn and learn, and can do things, but like a jigsaw puzzle,
there's still a couple of pieces that won't fit properly.  If that's
the case, one day they'll all click into place and you'll wonder why
you ever had a problem.

I don't do maths, my first degree is in Classics so you won't find me
saying that maths is essential to programming.  I look on it more as
problem solving and combine this with the refusal to let a dumb machine
beat me.  I enjoy it - so much so that I've reverted to being a genuine
techie again without any management involvement and that's after nearly
thirty years in the game now.

If you like programming, if you like problem solving, then just keep
plugging away until it all clicks into place (and it will), and try not
to be discouraged by class tests - all that tests do is test your
recall, not your programming skill.  I know it happened for me when I
spent a couple of days away from the learning with the then lady in my
life and, presumably, the old brain was still ticking away in the
background sorting out all of the information while we were
concentrating on... well, er, concentrating on other things.

Another thing you can do is ask for help here with your homework.  If
you show us what you've done that won't work, we'll  be only too happy
to help with pointers, and heavy clues and sometimes even answers.  We
only get uppity when students expect us to do all the work for them.
Oh, and they sometimes get upset when I threaten to code an ALTER
statement for old times' sake or when someone...

Certainly, if you feel that you can do it, keep plugging away and you
won't regret it.  The work has given me the chance to see the world and
it continues to put food on the table while I pursue other things, not
to mention my continued working travels.  And I found a nice newsgroup
here too.


Charles F Hankel
swanning around for six months in Dublin's fair city
```

```
That is the whole point of 'learning'.  You do what you think is write based
on what you can put together from class, then have it evaluated by others
to point out inconsistencies in placement, summarization, logic flow.....

There has always been many ways to the same end, just some are 
more correct than others.  Some use advanced tricks to get there
and only the most adept will be able to come in behind to maintenance it.
Others will use more simplistic processes and clearly defined field and
procedure names for the benefit of future coding maintenance.

In real life there thousands of programs written without regard for 
who has to follow the logic and maintain it in the future.
I should know as I have been working some 20 years and been thrown 
into some of the worst programs some demented soul could ever dream up.
Programs that may have started out some 30 years ago as nice neat 
structured systems have been maintained by so many different levels
of developers over time are also a challenge to deal with.

Sorry for my tirade here.
```

```
Deek,

Programming requires one to think like a computer.

The first step is to understand how computers work.

You can't really write a program if you don't understand what is actually
happening on the machine.

You're problem has nothing to do with CoBOL.  All languages are basically
the same. Sequence, condition, iteration.  That's it.

I would suggest getting yourself a book on computer architecture and
starting there.  Maybe try learning assembler.

You can do it if you try.

Jeff
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: discouraged with programming

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** Liam Devlin <liamddd@optonline.net>
- **Date:** 2000-12-09T23:43:08+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A32C36D.255DDC02@optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<20001208135334.05994.00003773@ng-ck1.aol.com> <20001208233021.25514.00002316@ng-ba1.aol.com> <QUpY5.4495$Sl.232521@iad-read.news.verio.net> <90u50r01jt1@enews4.newsguy.com>`

```
Jeff Baynard wrote:
> 
> Deek,
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> the same. Sequence, condition, iteration.  That's it.

I've long thought that the secret to programming is decision making and
loop control (and I wouldn't dissent if you argued that loop control is
a subset of decision making). If you get these right you'll write good
programs, fiddling with the output is minor compared to logic errors. I
coded Assembler for years (18), helping COBOL programmers debug their
programs; and my approach was to ask the programmer: 1. what does the
program do that it shouldn't, or 2. what doesn't it do that it should?
This alone solved many problems and got us focused on what was really
wrong. Some people tend to wander when they get into debugging mode.

If you're having trouble getting totals and/or dates to appear where you
want them, have you drawn a picture of your output and coded the output
data structure from that? If you're just starting, you probably need to
draw a flowchart or write pseudo code to describe the program before you
start actually coding it. I have to have a clear picture in my mind as
to what a new program is going to do before I start on it. If it's a
complex one, I'll draw flowcharts, decision tables, etc., whatever I
need until it is clear, then I can code it.

HTH,
LiamD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: discouraged with programming

- **From:** hankelie@my-deja.com
- **Date:** 2000-12-12T14:37:52+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<915d7v$dqo$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`
- **References:** `<90r1hi$mhc$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <20001208135334.05994.00003773@ng-ck1.aol.com>`

```
In article <20001208135334.05994.00003773@ng-ck1.aol.com>,
  deek40@aol.com (Deek40) wrote:
> My main problem i see with my COBOL programs is i get stuck where to
put what
> code for instance I had a program where we had to disply the date in
the header
> and I had part of the date in the wrong spot, another instance I had
my total
> line quantities in the wrong order, people say try being more
careful, but when
> i am doing it I think it is write, until someone points out thats
wrong then i
> am like duh, i should have caught that.  I am thinking of taking my
program
> that i know is write after many days of help from you guys/gals and
just plain
> reading everything I can about the part I am having trouble with and
going line
> by line and find out exactly what each peice of code does and the
reason it is
> in the spot it is.
> Thanks everyone,
> Derrick
>
> PS. When i wrote my initial post i was pissed off, discouraged and
working on a
> 10 page paper for WRT 100, so i wasnt in the best of moods.


Ah, we all know about this sort of thing; it's par for the course with
programming.  Years ago, we used to be lucky to get a single compile or
test run in a day because of the poor availability of system resources
back then.

So, instead of relying on the system to sort out our errors, we would
do most of it by desk checking our code and test data.  Luckily for me,
in my current shop, we can get hardcopy and it's a hell of a lot easier
to read on paper than on the display monitor.  The trick with reading
through the code is to forget that it was perfect when you coded it
(we're all that good!) and just read it for its own sake.

Often the mistakes stand out like the balls on a greyhound and you
wonder how you missed them the first time around.  At other times, it
might take some reading.

Something else I noticed with academics si that they like to
overcomplicate the code and almost encourage their students to do so.
If you can keep your statements down to a minimum, breaking up the long
ones into shorter ones and making use of paragraphs, you'll find that
it's easier to spot mistakes and correct them.

For instance, I won't code an IF statement of more than about five or
six lines in total.  I won't nest IF statements to more than one sub-
level.  It's all short chunks - there's no way that anyone can justify
to me the need to write massively complex statements other than their
desire to overcomplicate the maintainer's task more than
necessary.  "KISS - keep it simple, stupid" is as good a guide as any
for programming.


Charles F Hankel
(Bill Clinton just drove past me twice here in Dublin
 and the swine didn't even offer to buy me a beer.)


Sent via Deja.com http://www.deja.com/
Before you buy.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: discouraged with programming

```
Ditto, Charles.

Some of our program have page-long IF statments which are basically
incomprehensible.

Don't try to solve the whole problem at once. "chunck it." Write in "chunks" -
small, specialized (they do ONLY one thing each) paragraphs.  And remember,
just like your term papers, think in terms of drafts.  i.e. don't expect to get
it right the first time and you won't blind yourself to the bugs so much.

Charles, as for Bill offering you a beer - you didn't show him your thong.

Bob Jones
```

```
This is why we, i.e., my application, which is global in scope,
expresses time military style, no confusion (and all documents specify
that it's NYC time (where the systems are located)). Older software uses
MMDDYYYY or DDMMYYYY for dates, depending on local usage; but new
software uses the "dd MMM yyyy" format.

LiamD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Time from 24 hr to US

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-09T18:38:11-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<90uj4u$ee$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<20001207005738.18787.00001411@ng-cn1.aol.com> <fIVX5.71$U4.5241@newsread1.prod.itd.earthlink.net> <3a325c62$0$1510$7ea90a65@news.netdirect.net> <3A32C513.5A7D263@optonline.net>`

```
Getting back to COBOL <G>

 It should be noted that both ACCEPT FROM TIME and FUNCTION CURRENT-DATE
support "00:00" thru "23:59" values and *not* "00:01" thru "24:00" as a
valid times.  HOWEVER, J4 and WG4 have "discussed" the fact that the *new*
intrinsic function (in the draft Standard) "allows" for (but does not
REQUIRE) support for "24:00" as a returned value from the Locale-Time
function - in the case where a "locale" is defined allowing for 2400.
```

#### ↳ Re: Time from 24 hr to US

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2000-12-11T07:50:41-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A34E9C1.B8E0179A@brazee.net>`
- **References:** `<20001207005738.18787.00001411@ng-cn1.aol.com> <fIVX5.71$U4.5241@newsread1.prod.itd.earthlink.net>`

```


Jerry P wrote:

> Remember, to us purists, 1200 hours cannot be resolved to either AM or
> PM while 0000 hours is either AM or PM.

To language purists, our digital 12 hour watches are wrong twice a day.
They never say 12:00 M or 12:00 MM, or even 12 noon or midnight, but
instead have the oxymoron of 12:00 AM, which means 12:00 in the time
before noon. or 12:00 PM which means 12:00 in the afternoon.

While 00:00 hours doesn't mean either AM or PM, it unambiguously means
midnight, as does 24:00.

But even the term midnight is ambiguous when we have customers in
different time zones.  I watched a football game where they announced "60
minutes will immediately follow the game except on the west coast when it
will be seen on its regular time".  As a customer of the network, I
resented the fact that they forgot about my Rocky Mountain time, where it
also would be seen on its regular time. - Always be aware of the customer.

On a different note - with digital clocks, will the words widdershins and
deasil make a comeback?
```

## Thread: variable length records — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`VSAM, files, sorting`](topics.md#files)_

### Re: variable length records

- **From:** "Jerry P" <jerryp@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-08T00:27:58+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<iWVX5.132$U4.8728@newsread1.prod.itd.earthlink.net>`
- **References:** `<HG9X5.2295$jo.16777708@tomcat.sk.sympatico.ca> <9SaX5.1953$aO1.117854@cletus.bright.net> <uebX5.2309$jo.29622333@tomcat.sk.sympatico.ca> <jvyyrzubevmbagrfvasbezngvpnpbz.g54hsb0.pminews@news.wanadoo.es> <7xbX5.2311$jo.29557061@tomcat.sk.sympatico.ca>`

```

"mike" <mikman@home.com> wrote in message
news:7xbX5.2311$jo.29557061@tomcat.sk.sympatico.ca...
> No not on a mainframe, just running a dos application to
> convert some data. I'm just trying to read a sequential file
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> process it that way, thats for sure.

You'll never find it. The CRLF is not transferred to memory during an
ordinary sequential read. Further, the read is terminated at the CRLF
and any unfilled portions of the requested record are blank-filled by
the read operation.

Here's what do:

FD  MYFILE.
01  MYREC.
   02  MYHEAD        PIC X(222).
   02  MYBIG.
      05  MYMEDIUM
         10  MYSMALL  PIC X(37).
         10  FILLER        PIC X(29).
      05  FILLER           PIC X(45).

READ MYFILE.
(Use MYHEAD to deduce whether you have only the header, MYBIG,
MYMEDIUM, or MYSMALL record)
(process whichever)
```

<a id="t-1a3162dd26"></a>
## Thread: variable length records — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`VSAM, files, sorting`](topics.md#files)_

### Re: variable length records

- **From:** rjones1005@aol.com (RJones1005)
- **Date:** 2000-12-08T01:43:40+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<20001207204340.14301.00000073@ng-fw1.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<HG9X5.2295$jo.16777708@tomcat.sk.sympatico.ca>`

```
try this
FD UNI-CAT-FILE RECORD CONTAINS 333 characters
01	MY-RECORD.
	05	STANDARD-RECORD.
		10	STANDARD-DATA	PIC X(222).
		10	 VARIABLE-STUFF	PIC X(111).
	05	FUNNY-RECORD REDEFINES STANDARD-RECORD
		10	FUNNY-STANDARD-DATA	PIC X(222)
		10	FUNNY-DATA		PIC  X(32)
		10	FILLER				PIC  X(79)
	05	ODD-RECORD REDEFINES STANDARD-RECORD
		10 ODD-STANDARD-DATA		PIC X(222)
		10	ODD-DATA			PIC X(64)
		10	FILLER				PIC X(47)

It really doesn't matter that your record may be fewer characters as long as
the FD is set up for "worst case". Now your only challenge is to figure out
which "template" (REDEFINE) maps to the record you just read. We do that with a
special indicator character as part of the record key.
```

---

```
>start out with HH:MM in 24 hour time.  If HH < 12, it's AM, otherwise, it's
>PM.  In addition, if HH > 12, subtract 12 from it.

Sorry, that is not correct.

0000 = 12:00 PM (midnight)
0001 = 12:01 AM
..
1159 = 11:59 AM
1200 = 12:00 AM (noon)
1201 = 12:01 PM
..
2359 = 11:59 PM
```

```
Mygawdalmighty no!
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Time from 24 hr to US

- **From:** "Jerry P" <jerryp@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-09T17:46:50+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<eeuY5.5356$U4.554026@newsread1.prod.itd.earthlink.net>`
- **References:** `<20001207005738.18787.00001411@ng-cn1.aol.com> <90nah7$3944$1@newssvr06-en0.news.prodigy.com> <3a30e139$0$78095$a05e1490@news.netdirect.net> <fteY5.50424$II2.4538054@newsread2.prod.itd.earthlink.net> <3a325bc2$0$1510$7ea90a65@news.netdirect.net>`

```

"Doug Miller" <dlmiller@netdirect.net

> >
> >On the other hand, 0000 hours is 12:00 AM or 12:00 PM - take your
> >pick.

> False. 0000 is 12:00 PM or 12:00 midnight, but not under any
circumstances is
> it 12:00 AM -- that's noon.
>
> Guess you weren't paying too much attention in grade school.

Actually, I was so smart I skipped grade school. Sorry about yours.

P.M. = post merdiem = after noon.
A.M. = ante merdiem = before noon.

0000 hours is, therefore, 12 hours 'post merdiem'- after noon or 12
hours 'ante merdiem' - before noon. Take your pick. But if you do
choose one over the other, half your readers will be confused. Better
to use "12:00 midnight".
```

```
See < http://sciastro.astronomy.net/sci.astro.3.FAQ >. This is
Part 3 of Frequently Asked Questions of < news:sci.astro >.

An excerpt of that FAQ is shown below.

"Subject: C.02 What are all those different kinds of time?
Author: Paul Schlyter <pausch@saaf.se>,
Markus Kuhn <Markus.Kuhn@cl.cam.ac.uk>,
Paul Eggert <eggert@twinsun.com>

"...  (In strict English construction, 12:00 cannot be given
either an A.M. = ante meridiem or P.M. = post meridiem
designation, but it has become common to use  12 A.M
to mean midnight and 12 P.M. to mean noon.  In traditional
English, 12 M. = meridies means _noon_; nowadays one
is just as likely to see 12 M. = midnight and 12 N. = noon.)"
------------------
Rick Smith
```

```
There is no "24:00" on a 24-hour clock. 23:59 is followed by 00:00. And when 
using a twelve-hour clock, 11:59 is followed by 12:00, then 12:01, regardless 
of whether it's midnight or noon.
```

<a id="t-c2691942e5"></a>
## Thread: Timer Routine? — 3 msgs

### Timer Routine?

- **From:** "Kevin" <email@email.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-08T16:05:11+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<XE7Y5.410156$4d.52560553@news02.optonline.net>`

```
I have an application that uses a security key dongle. The key requires that
it be polled every 90 seconds to ensure that the application is still
active.

I am trying to determine the best method of accomplishing this without using
too many system resources.

I have thought about creating a thread that would check the time and after
90 seconds,  poll the key.
I also understand there is a Win API called "SLEEP" that would create a
pause that I could use in the thread that checks the key.

Has anyone ever done anything like this?
Does anyone know of the best way to do this without expending too many
resources?
Any ideas would be appreciated.

Thanks,
Kevin
```

#### ↳ Re: Timer Routine?

- **From:** "donald tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-08T11:44:26-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<90r31s$sdi$1@news.igs.net>`
- **References:** `<XE7Y5.410156$4d.52560553@news02.optonline.net>`

```
I responded in the Flexus list, Kevin, but am including it here
as it may be of interest to a few not using SP2 as well.

I am just setting the timer in the screens for polling.  That will force a
screen
exit with a "timer" keycode.  Since I am coding my screens as objects, the
routine to service the "interupt" (even though it is really a poll) is
buried in
the object.  The structure is as follows.

The screen is an object of class "x", and is generated by SP2. A second
class (Y) inherits all the screens, and manages the common elements.
Screens are opened by invoking them by name from class Y, which sets
screen position, and handles the "help key", as well as any timed events.
Since all screens are of that class, it is common for all screens in the
system.  Note that this means *every* screen in the system must have
the timer set, but that can be done in class Y, as part of the open.

It works very well for most stuff, provided you have nice big buffers
for any serial input.  I am polling three RS-232 devices plus the keyboard
for all my screens.  Input can be from any of the four devices.  One
RS-232 looks like a second keyboard, and actually "fakes" SP2 return
keys quite invisibly.  The other two RS-232 are a card reader and a scale.
The scale is just read, but the card reader fills in a screen field, then
"presses" the "APPLY" button on a screen.  The high level Cobol code
does not even know that it was not operator input, which makes it nice
and clean to code.

 Kevin wrote in message ...
>I have an application that uses a security key dongle. The key requires
that
>it be polled every 90 seconds to ensure that the application is still
>active.
>
>I am trying to determine the best method of accomplishing this without
using
>too many system resources.
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

#### ↳ Re: Timer Routine?

- **From:** Fim Wästberg <fim.wastberg@fimator.se>
- **Date:** 2000-12-09T12:49:47+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A321C5A.C9A64B61@fimator.se>`
- **References:** `<XE7Y5.410156$4d.52560553@news02.optonline.net>`

```
If you are using AcuCobol 32bit-version: CALL C$SLEEP USING "90".
Will not use "too many system resources".
```

---

<a id="t-eed1cbcc48"></a>
## Thread: NE 3.0 setting standard-printer — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards)_

### Re: NE 3.0 setting standard-printer

- **From:** James Gavan <jjgavan@home.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-09T02:22:18+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3A3197E4.63498AC1@home.com>`
- **References:** `<909jun$u0c$1@riker.addcom.de>`

```


Andreas Nehls wrote:

> Hi,
> I am working with NE 3.0 and i am used to read and set variables
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Andreas,

If you upgrade to N/E Version 3.1 you will see that PC_PRINT routines
now have the ability to set the default printer.
------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Sets the default printer for all PC_PRINTER_ routines.

call "PC_PRINTER_DEFAULT_NAME" using by value     flags
                                     by reference printer-name
                                     returning    status-code
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
<catrysse@club-internet.fr> writes:

>So, my (dummy) question could be: is there a way to access these old cobol
>data files and manipulate them with an ODBC application. All I know is that
>this program has been made with acu-cobol in the late 90's.
>Please, give me enlightement...
>

It is highly unlikely that you will find any ODBC type methods to access
the files without first having the original file layouts to aide in defining
the 
tables(record definitions) such that a generic flat file ODBC engine 'might'
be able to extract the data.  If there are any packed or  binary fields then
you run into even more difficulty as the data storage may not be easily
mapped to an ODBC type int/long/....
This type of question continues to come up in monthly cycles.  You might 
want to search the newsgroup from some archive location for more immediate
access to other potential solutions.
```

```

who makes it or how to use it, I just know it allows you to access COBOL files
directly, through ODBC.
```

```
Thane, I don't think this problem is restricted to the language product.
I've seen any number of systems where the file structures are not properly
documented, and it doesn't matter what language product was used to create
them.

(Shameless plug alert #1) At least there is a freeware document at
http://www.flexus.com/ebd2asc.html  which , while originally written to deal
with EBCDIC-ASCII issues, can also be useful for the "generic" COBOL data
conversion challenge.

(Shameless plug alert #2). And for those working in the Windows (32-bit)
environment, I have a freeware DLL and conversion program which can convert
COBOL data to IEEE data (also does EBCDIC-ASCII conversions with proper
respect for BCD and binary fields). I've sent out about 20 of these for
evaluation, but not one person has given me the courtesy of the requested
"comments on this product." I think I may start changing for the conversion
kit, with a 100% refund on receipt of the analysis and maybe some
representative code in which the product was used.
```

```
when I've raised it you've always opposed it vehemently; has the advent of

other languages, too) there is a sad lack of proper documentation of file
structures. One reason why I advocate the use of databases, which are
consistent and accessible by everybody.

I too have been encapsulating data access into COM objects, for both File
System and RDB data. It is a very good solution.

Pete.
```

```
> The father of CICS is Ben Riggins (IBM), and he headed a team of a few
people (also IBM)
> that designed and built a transaction processing system.  He was present
at the last CICS
> conference
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> When  Ben  was given  the  go-ahead  to  do  something with his idea, he
moved to Des
> Plaines, Illinois, which was the first development location for  CICS.
>
> Initially,  Ben  reported  to a manager (Skip White) who really had an
industry
> responsibility and he really didn't manage CICS.   The  first CICS
Development or Product
> Manager was Gerry Anderson.  Both Ben and Gerry have been retired from IBM
for some years
> now.
>
> Among Ben's small group initially were Gerry Hughes, to whom Ben  gave the
responsibility
> for the original master terminal (CSMT) and other transactions like that.
Carl Leinfelder
> was given  Terminal  Control, John  Lawrence  had Program Control (and the
introduction of
> COBOL and PL/I support in 1970), and Ray VanderVliet had File Control.
>
>
> It was later released as one of the first IBM program products.  In the
original original
> announcement letter (April 29, 1968) it was called PUCICS - Public
Utilities Customer
> Information System:
>
…[40 more quoted lines elided]…
> On July 8, 1969, CICS was born (Without the PU).  Also, it was announced
as a class II
> program (which means it was supposed to be free of charge), but when it
came around IBM
> made it a Program Product and charged money for it:
>
…[39 more quoted lines elided]…
>          Strike while the iron is hot.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: CICS for Cobol

<a id="t-79fdbc234c"></a>
## Thread: variable record-length in COBOL — 1 msgs

### Re: variable record-length in COBOL

- **From:** "Jeff Baynard" <union27@macconnect.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-09T15:13:34-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<90u3pd02mh1@enews3.newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<8vqg13$mmn$05$1@news.t-online.com> <90aoeq$2am$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <3A2BA4FA.CB89CCDA@brazee.net> <lm9X5.7426$2P3.548626@bgtnsc06-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```
Let's not forget that some environments have compression.

So if I have a 1500 byte fixed length record, but only use, on average, 80
bytes of that record, the rest of the record has some arbitrary values.
Like low values or spaces.

In our OS390 environment, at least when I write to tape, CA-1 compresses the
data (COMP=TRTCH).  So it may seem wasteful to use a fixed length record in
a situation like this, but in reality it is highly efficient.

In some instances, it may be helpful to use RECFM=VB.  For instance, weekly
our shop pulls back a 3 gigabyte (total) file from our stores.  This file
originates from a PC based environment (4690 OS) that produces variable
length records.  It is pulled back from the stores, then concatenated on one
of our SP nodes.  But we want the data stored on the mainframe.  At times,
our shop does not have 3 gigabytes readily available (mostly because of our
15 extent per volume, 3 volume standard; you know the drill, request too
much space and no volumes get selected, request too little and you go past
15*3 extents).  And I can't write to tape, because our FTP2 does not support
it (AFAIK).  So, by using RECFM=VB, I can get the FTP job to run more
reliably and use less space.  Obviously after the file is FTPed, I make a
fixed length dataset on tape and uncatalog the DASD space.

Of course, there are certainly better ways to go about the above example.
Why not pull back one file per store and keep them as seperate datasets
(instead of one 3-gig dataset).  This was certainly contemplated.  But we
didn't have the time to write some dynamic allocation stuff to do the
FTPing.  Plus one file is easier to manage than 184 files.  So at the time,
the one 3-gig file was a better choice.  By going to RECFM=VB, I was able to
make it work.

So this goes full circle back to the point that there are always many
different ways to do something.  As long as it is reliable, clear and
reasonably speedy than there's nothing wrong with doing something a certain
way.  In some cases, variable length records may be the best way to do
something.

Jeff
```

---

### Re: VSAM record-length

- **From:** "mangogwr" <mangogrower@telocity.co>
- **Date:** 2000-12-09T16:27:24-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<pqxY5.19327$C2.5011649@newsrump.sjc.telocity.net>`
- **References:** `<901ecb$q6p$1@rohrpostix.uta4you.at> <906ubt$5ekv$1@ID-39920.news.dfncis.de> <Ag+EuAA8IWK6EwjP@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> <90egl3$jac$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <90en8q$teo8$1@ID-39920.news.dfncis.de> <cyCW5.1854$DR1.293396@paloalto-snr1.gtei.net> <2rDW5.3160$C2.1115397@newsrump.sjc.telocity.net> <90f3l1$k13$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
Correct, it won't work BUT not for the reason you stated.
(I just tested it because) I had forgotten that for Hi-Level Languages, VSAM
has
no such concept as 'buffering' like for QSAM.
Instead for COBOL the read is always in 'MOVE MODE' meaning Get the
record, MOVE it to my area.

IBM COBOL creates an ACB similar to Assembler ACBs.
The varying length will return the correct length ALWAYS for VSAM,
99.9999% for QSAM - Compiler bugs since 97 have caused internal corruption
in the COBOL QSAM runtime with Variable data of certain lengths in certain
orders.
I have encountered it several times and IBM had to give up and the ONLY work
around
was 'READ INTO'. Even compiling with 'IF some-condition, READ xxx INTO
ELSE READ xxx MOVE xx-rec  to ' and setting the condition to FALSE,
the reads returned the correct LRECL.

For VSAM, however, the 01 address will point to the 'USER WORK AREA'.
16 bytes further down from the address in the FCB holding the '01 ' / USER
WORK AREA
address, is the 2byte LRECL as unsigned binary.

There is no COBOL AVAILABLE way to get it OTHER than the RECORD IS VARYING
IN SIZE
nn to nn DEPENDING UPON xxx.  Even if you know that the TGT address  +hex
110 is the address
of the first FCB and +4 from that address is the next and so on, COBOL so
far doesn't provide
'POINTER Math', NEITHER does it allow you to get the TGT address.

If you have FIXED length records with different fixed lengths, you MUST
create them
with an IDENTIFIER in each record that indicates the type / record-length.

AND sometimes, when someone says different length records, they mean
a FILE of records that have more than one length, but that the records
themselves
aren't 'variable length', in that they lack an ODO (Occurs x to n times
Depending On) variable.

when
> the 01-level is a COPY of the currently read record.
```

```
  <snip>

> If you have FIXED length records with different fixed lengths, you MUST
> create them

If you mean an FD with something like:

FD  .... various phrases

01  Type1  Pic X(10).
01  Type2  Pic X(100).
01  Type3  Pic  X(1000).

Then the RECORD VARYING IN SIZE from 10 to 1000 depending on WS-item most
*definitely* will contain the size of each record that is read.  (This is
Not just valid if there is an ODO in one of the 01-levels).  And you
certainly do NOT need any "field" within each type of record to tell which
is read.

NOTE WELL:
  I am talking about the RECORD VARYING IN SIZE phrase (of the '85
Standard - IBM VS COBOL II R3.0 and later) and *not* about an OCCURS
DEPENDING ON phrase within one of the 01-levels.
```

```
You mean you've never seen this:

            SELECT FILEIN1        ASSIGN TO FILEIN1.
           SELECT FILEIN2        ASSIGN TO FILEIN2.
           SELECT FILEOUT        ASSIGN TO FILEOUT.
       DATA DIVISION.
      ******************************************************************
       FILE SECTION.
      ******************************************************************
       FD  FILEIN1
           BLOCK CONTAINS 0 RECORDS.

       01  FILEIN1-RACHRG-REC          PIC X(0081).
       01  FILEIN1-RAADDR-REC          PIC X(0200).

       01  RCF-RECO.
           05  RCF-RA-NUMBER           PIC S9(015) COMP-3.
           05  RCF-REC-CODE            PIC  X(001).
           05  RCF-REC-DATA            PIC  X(1800).


These are all 'FORMAT 2' FDs.  Where the record lengths are different,
yet records themselves are FIXED length.  The OS and Lang. Env both need
to know how many bytes each has, and how large a block is, but they are
NOT variable length record, since 1) there is not an ODO, 2) based upon
some value in a specific location, the records can be determined to have
specified lengths.
================
5.2.5.2 Format 2

Format 2 specifies the number of character positions for either fixed-length
or variable-length records.
Fixed-length records are obtained when all 01 record description entry
lengths are the same.  The Format 2  RECORD CONTAINS clause is never
required, because the minimum and maximum record lengths are determined from
the record description entries.

 PICTURE 143 Under AIX, OS/2, and Windows, the Format 2 RECORD clause is not
supported for line
sequential files. PICTURE 144

+--- Format 2 -----------------------------------------------------------+
�                                                                        �
� >>--RECORD----------------integer-4--TO--integer-5------------------>< �
�             +-CONTAINS-+                            +-CHARACTERS-+     �
�                                                                        �
+------------------------------------------------------------------------+
integer-4
integer-5
    Must be unsigned integers.  Integer-4 specifies the size of the smallest
data record, and integer-5
    specifies the size of the largest data record.


SO, OK you aren't required by the Language or OS to create the
record_type_Id, BUT if you don't
then you are just making it harder, because when you are using this type, it
is usually because
your records do in deed have 'fixed lengths', you just happen to have
several different fixed lengths.
Without the IDentifer, it is much harder to SORT off the ones you want, (yes
you can sort based upon
actual record length, but why when you can use one byte to ID the record?

Since FORMAT two does not have the RECORD IS VARYING IN SIZE, yet it creates
a V/VB format
file, my statement that this is a file of different length fixed length
records.  Once you verify the record_ID,
that type of record will stay @ n bytes.

ALSO, there is no 'DEPENDING ON' item to set the record length on WRITES or
to check on
READS, therefore, the records themselves do NOT vary in size.  In Format 3,
for instance

RECORD CONTAINS 62 to 4096 CHARACTERS DEPENDING ON WS-VAR_REC-LEN.

Failure to properly set the DO item, will truncate records OR waste space
with leftovers from
prior longer records.

In Format 2, when you specify wihich record to WRITE, the Language sets the
length, and
SINCE the DEPENDING ON item is not available, if you DO NOT provide the
internal
Record_ID, then you have to figure out which type you just read in.

news:90u9li$7gd$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net...
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: VSAM record-length

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-10T14:59:22-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<910qoo$v20$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<901ecb$q6p$1@rohrpostix.uta4you.at> <906ubt$5ekv$1@ID-39920.news.dfncis.de> <Ag+EuAA8IWK6EwjP@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> <90egl3$jac$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <90en8q$teo8$1@ID-39920.news.dfncis.de> <cyCW5.1854$DR1.293396@paloalto-snr1.gtei.net> <2rDW5.3160$C2.1115397@newsrump.sjc.telocity.net> <90f3l1$k13$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net> <pqxY5.19327$C2.5011649@newsrump.sjc.telocity.net> <90u9li$7gd$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net> <DARY5.23985$C2.5718322@newsrump.sjc.telocity.net>`

```
When you use a "format 2" RECORD clause and there are multiple length
records, then you *must* define the physical file as RECFM=V or RECFM=VB.
This is INDEED a "variable length file" - although it contains no ODO.  This
is "simply" the way the language is defined.

Once a file has been created with a format 2 record contains clause (whether
it is VSAM or QSAM or SAM on VSE), then it is *always* possible to *read*
that file using the RECORD VARYING IN SIZE format or the RECORD clause.
(Again this assumes an '85 Standard compiler - this was NOT available with
OS/VS COBOL or the first 2 releases of VS COBOL II).  This means that it is
ABSOLUTELY POSITIVELY possible to get the length of each record on a READ
statement.  You are correct that if you keep to a "format 2" RECORD clause
in the program that READs the file, that you can't get the length - but that
is like saying that if you don't use either the MULTIPLY or COMPUTE verb,
you can't do multiplication.  The ABILITY to get this information in in the
language and it WORKS.  It works no matter what type of RECORD clause was
used to create the file - or even if the file was created in a non-COBOL
application. (By the way, it works with *both* VS COBOL II, R3.0 or later
*or* with an LE-enabled COBOL - this is part of the language and has nothing
to do with the compiler or run-time used).

NOTE WELL: from the definition that you gave for format2
  "Fixed-length records are obtained when all 01 record description entry
lengths are the same. "

It simply is not true to EVER speak of a "fixed length file" when there are
records of different lengths (whether via an ODO or multiple 01-levels).
```

## Thread: VSAM record-length — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`VSAM, files, sorting`](topics.md#files)_

### Re: VSAM record-length

- **From:** JOSEPH SHANE <jbwshane@nac.net>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
My understanding of the VSAM RDW was that it would appear preceding the
logical record when the VSAM KSDS was reeled off in logical order onto a
tape, thus adding the four bytes of the RDW to the physical length of
the backup.  In its sequential form, wouldn't the LRECL be thus exposed
(through the RDW which is now the first four bytes of the physical
record)?
```

#### ↳ Re: VSAM record-length

- **From:** "Willem Clements" <willem@horizontes-informatica.com>
- **Date:** 2000-12-10T18:22:41+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<jvyyrzubevmbagrfvasbezngvpnpbz.g5do010.pminews@news.wanadoo.es>`
- **References:** `<901ecb$q6p$1@rohrpostix.uta4you.at> <906ubt$5ekv$1@ID-39920.news.dfncis.de> <Ag+EuAA8IWK6EwjP@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> <90egl3$jac$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <jvyyrzubevmbagrfvasbezngvpnpbz.g51i0q0.pminews@news.wanadoo.es> <3A32CF89.E1D825E8@nac.net>`

```
On Sat, 09 Dec 2000 19:34:17 -0500, JOSEPH SHANE wrote:

>My understanding of the VSAM RDW was that it would appear preceding the
>logical record when the VSAM KSDS was reeled off in logical order onto a
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

A variable length file on tape always contains length fields preceeding the
records, independent of the origin of the data. These four bytes are not the
same as a VSAM RDF. VSAM RDF's are just three bytes long and appear in pairs,
one containing lengths and the other one a record count. The only way to
access those RDF's is to process the VSAM file by CI.

Willem Clements
Brainbench MVP for COBOL II
http://www.brainbench.com
```

## Thread: Problem with COBOL and DB2/NT — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases)_

### Re: Problem with COBOL and DB2/NT

- **From:** "Joseph Brancato" <JBRANCATO@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2000-12-10T03:31:00+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<UNCY5.23045$0r2.851347@bgtnsc07-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<3A2CBBE2.9C73F365@tid.es>`

```
I hope that the below is applicable to Visual COBOL.  Whether it is or not
let me know if you have the time to respond back.  I use UDB 6.1 NT with
database stored on a Server with MicroFocus COBOL MFE (Mainframe Express).
You may be encountering the basic restriction of UDB that you may not be
aware of regarding BInding of "Packages" (or Collections).  In UDB the first
person that binds a specific program becomes the owner of the package and is
subsequently the only userid that has the access rights to re-bind (the
pgm's Package) and is the only userid that has the access to even execute
the pgm (the pgm's package).  Therefore, the procedure to follow is that
when a new cobol UDB program is created and compiled for the first time, the
first person that compile-binds it must be the person (userid) to issue the
below (2) commands in Control Center:

                        GRANT Package-Name  BIND PUBLIC
                        GRANT Package-Name  EXECUTE PUBLIC

    Note - In above commands "Package-Name" is composed of
"CollectionName.ProgramName".
                For Example:

                        GRANT ABCSYSTEM.PGM1  BIND PUBLIC
                        GRANT ABCSYSTEM.PGM1  EXECUTE PUBLIC

Note: Only the first person that complie-Binds the program has the access to
grant access to other users for re-binding or even executing the program.
The exception of course is that the DBA who has ADMIN access rights can also
issue the above UDB commands to grant re-binding and execute access of the
Package to other userids (a.k.a via access to "PUBLIC").

Note: You should specify a Package "Collection-Name" when you bind your
programs (I do not know how you do this in VisualAge, since I don't use this
package.  If you don't specify a Collection Name when binding, I believe UDB
will subsitute the Userid of person binding as the actual Collection Name to
be used for the program (this is then even more complicated to keep track
of).  You should come up with a Package "Collection Name (a.k.a. Collection
Schema) which is unique to your system and if you do not authority to
"Create Implicit Schema Name" then the DBA will have to create the
Collection "Schema" Name you decide on.  Then you should enter the
"Collection Name" for your application system into Visual Age for Binding
your programs after compilation (I do not know Visual Age, so I am just
assuming it has an option to specify a Collection name.  If your binding by
issuing a command to UDB in command center or caomand window there is
definately a way to specify it, but I could not tell you how since I enter
my collection name in MicroFocus COBOL MFE and it sends to UDB when
interafcing with UDB for binding operation).




"Jesus Marcos Martin" <jesusmm@tid.es> wrote in message
news:3A2CBBE2.9C73F365@tid.es...
> Hi,
>
…[36 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```
I received the following from one of my "usually reliable sources" at IBM.
I don't know how helpful it is - but I pass it on anyway.

"They have to make the 'integrated precompiler' part of  DB2 available to
the user who is compiling, I am not sure if those services can be shared or
not.  They could have a workaround by using the precompiler as a separate
step of compiling, but it probably has similar restrictions.  If it does
work for remote users to use the precompiler, then there is either a setup
problem for the integrated precompiler or maybe each user is trying to use a
different userid or
something.

I would recommend more experiments to narrow the problems down, by comparing
the 2 different methods of compiling programs with SQL statements.    I
would read the
DB2 manuals and also see if the remote users can RUN the programs that
access
the DB2 Data Base.  It may be a general remote access to DB2 problem rather
than
a remote precompiler/integrated precompiler problem."
```

```
Achim Schupeta <schupeta@canach.muc.sdm.de> wrote
> could you please comment and give a recommendation for
> the following situation concerning programming guidelines and
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> --------------------------------------------------------------

> Here are the arguments of our customer to introduce that rule:

Only the first three arguments are related to the intrinsic merit (or
not) of lack of qualification. The rest are related to your particular
environment and have nothing to do with COBOL.

Qualification can be useful if you don't overdo it (CUST-NO OF AAA OF BBB
....).
Prefixes and suffixes often make for difficult to read code.
A good test of the "goodness" of a name is if you can read it
loud into a telephone and the recipient can know what you mean.

Personally, I never use qualification, but I have seen cases
where is was positively useful. The real issue is that at every
step the choice is made such that the program becomes
easier to maintain (by somebody else!!). Blindly following
a rule may not always be best.

Sorry that this answer is not as clear cur as you might have wanted,
but such is the beast that no iron-clad rule always suffices...
```

```
Achim Schupeta wrote:
```
